reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes=1000):
        """
        EfficientNetB2 architecture implementation.

        :param num_classes: The number of output classes (default is 1000 for ImageNet).
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        # Define the EfficientNetB2 architecture components
        self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(3, 32, kernel_size=3, stride=2, padding=1, bias=False)
        self.bn1 = nn.BatchNorm2d(32)
        self.relu = nn.ReLU(inplace=True)
        
        # Define the MBConv blocks
        self.mbconv1 = self._make_mbconv_block(32, 96, 1, 3)
        self.mbconv2 = self._make_mbconv_block(96, 144, 2, 6)
        self.mbconv3 = self._make_mbconv_block(144, 192, 2, 6)
        self.mbconv4 = self._make_mbconv_block(192, 288, 2, 6)
        self.mbconv5 = self._make_mbconv_block(288, 384, 1, 6)
        
        # Final layers
        self.conv_final = nn.Conv2d(384, 1408, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False)
        self.bn_final = nn.BatchNorm2d(1408)
        self.avgpool = nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1, 1))
        self.fc = nn.Linear(1408, num_classes)
    
    def _make_mbconv_block(self, in_channels, out_channels, stride, expand_ratio):
        """
        Helper function to create a MBConv block.

        :param in_channels: Number of input channels.
        :param out_channels: Number of output channels.
        :param stride: Stride for the depthwise convolution.
        :param expand_ratio: Expansion ratio for the MBConv block.
        :return: A sequential container of layers forming the MBConv block.
        """
        layers = []
        expanded_channels = in_channels * expand_ratio
        
        # Expansion phase
        if expand_ratio != 1:
            layers.append(nn.Conv2d(in_channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False))
            layers.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(expanded_channels))
            layers.append(nn.ReLU(inplace=True))
        
        # Depthwise convolution
        layers.append(nn.Conv2d(expanded_channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size=3, stride=stride, padding=1, groups=expanded_channels, bias=False))
        layers.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(expanded_channels))
        layers.append(nn.ReLU(inplace=True))
        
        # Squeeze and Excitation
        layers.append(nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1, 1)))
        layers.append(nn.Conv2d(expanded_channels, expanded_channels // 4, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False))
        layers.append(nn.ReLU(inplace=True))
        layers.append(nn.Conv2d(expanded_channels // 4, expanded_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False))
        layers.append(nn.Sigmoid())
        
        # Output phase
        layers.append(nn.Conv2d(expanded_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False))
        layers.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels))
        
        return nn.Sequential(*layers)
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass of the EfficientNetB2 model.

        :param x: The input tensor, shape (batch_size, 3, 224, 224)
        :return: The output tensor, shape (batch_size, num_classes)
        """
        x = self.relu(self.bn1(self.conv1(x)))
        x = self.mbconv1(x)
        x = self.mbconv2(x)
        x = self.mbconv3(x)
        x = self.mbconv4(x)
        x = self.mbconv5(x)
        x = self.relu(self.bn_final(self.conv_final(x)))
        x = self.avgpool(x)
        x = torch.flatten(x, 1)
        x = self.fc(x)
        return x

# Test code
batch_size = 2
num_classes = 1000

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, 3, 224, 224)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_classes]

Simpler: we can manually compute folded weights for each conv+bn pair. For eval mode, `y = (x - mean)/sqrt(var+eps) * gamma + beta`. For a conv with weight W and bias b (here bias=False, so b=0), the fused weight is `W * (gamma / sqrt(var+eps))` and fused bias is `beta - mean * gamma / sqrt(var+eps)`. To beat it, I need a very efficient fused kernel that avoids intermediate memory allocations and keeps data in shared memory/registers as much as possible. The depthwise convolutions on 1x1 inputs with 3x3 kernel and stride 2 are essentially: For each channel c, output[b,c] = sum_{i,j} weight[c, i, j] * input[b, c] (since padding=1 makes input effectively 0-padded to 3x3, but with stride 2, only the center pixel matters? Actually on 1x1 input with padding 1 and kernel 3, stride 2: Input padded is 3x3. Output size = floor((1+2-3)/2)+1 = 1. The receptive field for the single output pixel covers the padded 3x3 area. The original 1x1 pixel is at center (1,1). So output = weight[c,0,1]*0 + weight[c,1,0]*0 + weight[c,1,1]*input + ... actually all other positions are 0 because padding is 0. So output[b,c] = weight[c,1,1] * input[b,c]. It's just a channel-wise scaling! This is crucial. Similarly, stride 1 on 1x1 input: Output size = floor((1+2-3)/1)+1 = 1. Output[b,c] = weight[c,1,1] * input[b,c] + ... again, only center pixel has data. So depthwise conv on 1x1 input is always just scaling by the center weight. And for the initial mbconv1, the depthwise conv IS on 112x112 spatial size, so it's a real depthwise convolution. And conv1 is a real conv on 224x224->112x112. So we have: 1. conv1: real conv 2. mbconv1: real expand conv, real depthwise conv, SE, output conv 3. mbconv2-5: 1x1 "conv" (linear), channel-wise scale, SE (just linear layers), output "conv" (linear) 4. final: 1x1 conv 5. fc: linear So essentially, the model is: - A small feature extractor (conv1 + mbconv1) - Followed by a purely MLP-like stack. This means we can massively optimize by: - Using PyTorch convs for the real convolutions (conv1, mbconv1 expand, mbconv1 depthwise, mbconv1 output). These are well-optimized by cuDNN. - For the rest (which is all 1x1), instead of launching many Conv2d kernels which call cuDNN with huge overhead, we can just do matrix multiplications or custom element-wise kernels. But wait, the reference implementation uses `nn.Conv2d` for everything. On 1x1 inputs, these are basically matrix multiplications but wrapped in cuDNN conv2d calls. The overhead might be significant. Yes, the output conv takes the output of Sigmoid, not the depthwise features. So the depthwise features at 112x112 are completely discarded after avgpool and SE! The entire 112x112 feature map is thrown away. Only the global average pooled vector goes through. This is a massive architecture bug, but it's the reference. So let's trace the exact data flow: - Input: [B,3,224,224] - conv1 -> [B,32,112,112] - bn1+relu - mbconv1: expand conv 1x1 (32->96) on 112x112 -> [B,96,112,112] bn+relu depthwise 3x3 (96->96,g=96) on 112x112 -> [B,96,112,112] bn+relu avgpool(1,1) -> [B,96,1,1] conv 1x1 (96->24) -> [B,24,1,1] relu conv 1x1 (24->96) -> [B,96,1,1] sigmoid conv 1x1 (96->96) -> [B,96,1,1] bn - mbconv2: expand conv 1x1 (96->576) on 1x1 -> [B,576,1,1] bn+relu depthwise 3x3 s=2 (576->576,g=576) on 1x1 -> [B,576,1,1] bn+relu avgpool -> [B,576,1,1] conv 1x1 (576->144) -> [B,144,1,1] relu conv 1x1 (144->576) -> [B,576,1,1] sigmoid conv 1x1 (576->144) -> [B,144,1,1] bn - mbconv3: similar, 144->864->192 - mbconv4: 192->1152->288 - mbconv5: 288->1728->384 - conv_final: 384->1408 on 1x1 - bn+relu - avgpool - flatten - fc: 1408->1000 So this is effectively: 1. `conv1` (real conv) on 224x224 2. `expand1` (real pointwise conv) on 112x112 3. `depthwise1` (real depthwise conv) on 112x112 4. For batch_size=2, and channels in the hundreds, each "conv" is actually a small GEMM (e.g., 576x144). cuDNN might not be the fastest for these tiny GEMMs. So a good optimization strategy: 1. As analyzed, depthwise conv on 1x1 with padding=1 and stride=2 or 1 can be reduced to scaling by the center weight of the kernel. For a depthwise conv with weight shape [C, 1, 3, 3], the center weight is at [:, 0, 1, 1]. The output for 1x1 input is `input[:, c] * weight[c, 0, 1, 1]`. Original: depthwise_conv(no bias) -> BN -> ReLU. Fused: multiply by center weight * gamma / std, then add fused bias, then ReLU. Let `w_fused = weight[:, 0, 1, 1] * gamma / std` Let `b_fused = beta - mean * gamma / std` Then `expand_out = relu(expand * w_fused + b_fused)`. No residual connections. This means we can definitely fuse the entire MLP tail into a single kernel. Input: [B, 3, 224, 224] Output: [B, 1000] This is a single end-to-end kernel. This would be ideal. But it requires implementing conv1, expand1, depthwise1 in CUDA. MACs per output: 9 (since groups=96). Total MACs: 1,204,224 * 9 = 10.8M. Total compute is ~60M MACs. At GPU compute capability of ~10 TFLOPS, this is ~6 microseconds at 100% utilization. Memory bandwidth will likely dominate: input is 224*224*3*4 = 600KB, output of conv1 is 112*112*32*4 = 1.6MB, output of expand1 is 112*112*96*4 = 4.8MB. Total memory traffic if fully materialized is ~8MB. At 1TB/s bandwidth, that's ~8 microseconds. So the total reference time for just these convs might be 20-50 microseconds. Actually, let's think more realistically: launching kernels has overhead (~5-10us per kernel). With ~40 layers (conv, bn, relu, etc.), that's ~40 kernels. At 10us each, that's 400us. Plus actual compute and memory traffic. So total reference time might be 0.5-1ms. To beat this, I need to reduce kernel launch count. Fusing conv+bn+relu reduces launches by ~2x. Let's calculate the parameters for the tail kernel: From mbconv1 depthwise output (call it `x`, [B,96,112,112]): Step A: Compute `v[b, c] = sum_h,w x[b, c, h, w] / (112*112)` Step B: mbconv1 tail: v1 = relu(v @ W_se1_1^T) # W_se1_1 shape [24, 96] v2 = sigmoid(v1 @ W_se1_2^T) # W_se1_2 shape [96, 24] v3 = v2 @ W_out1^T + b_out1 # W_out1 shape [96, 96] Step C: mbconv2: v4 = relu(v3 @ W_expand2^T + b_expand2) # W_expand2 [576, 96] v5 = v4 * w_dw2_fused + b_dw2_fused # element-wise, w_dw2_fused [576] v6 = relu(v5) v7 = relu(v6 @ W_se2_1^T) # W_se2_1 [144, 576] v8 = sigmoid(v7 @ W_se2_2^T) # W_se2_2 [576, 144] v9 = v8 @ W_out2^T + b_out2 # W_out2 [144, 576] Step D: mbconv3: v10 = relu(v9 @ W_expand3^T + b_expand3) # [864, 144] v11 = v10 * w_dw3_fused + b_dw3_fused # [864] v12 = relu(v11) v13 = relu(v12 @ W_se3_1^T) # [192, 864] v14 = sigmoid(v13 @ W_se3_2^T) # [864, 192] v15 = v14 @ W_out3^T + b_out3 # [192, 864] Step E: mbconv4: v16 = relu(v15 @ W_expand4^T + b_expand4) # [1152, 192] v17 = v16 * w_dw4_fused + b_dw4_fused # [1152] v18 = relu(v17) v19 = relu(v18 @ W_se4_1^T) # [288, 1152] v20 = sigmoid(v19 @ W_se4_2^T) # [1152, 288] v21 = v20 @ W_out4^T + b_out4 # [288, 1152] Step F: mbconv5: v22 = relu(v21 @ W_expand5^T + b_expand5) # [1728, 288] v23 = v22 * w_dw5_fused + b_dw5_fused # [1728] v24 = relu(v23) v25 = relu(v24 @ W_se5_1^T) # [384, 1728] v26 = sigmoid(v25 @ W_se5_2^T) # [1728, 384] v27 = v26 @ W_out5^T + b_out5 # [384, 1728] Step G: final v28 = relu(v27 @ W_final^T + b_final) # [1408, 384] Step H: FC v29 = v28 @ W_fc^T + b_fc # [1000, 1408] This is a lot of parameters. Implementation strategy for the custom tail kernel: - The kernel takes `float* input` which is [B, 96, 112, 112]. - It computes `v[96]` = mean over h,w for each channel. - Then it applies the sequence of linear transformations. - Weights and biases are passed as individual `float*` tensors. Actually, the channel sizes for each MBConv block are hardcoded in the model: - mbconv1: in=32, expanded=96, out=96, stride=1, ratio=3 - mbconv2: in=96, expanded=576, out=144, stride=2, ratio=6 - mbconv3: in=144, expanded=864, out=192, stride=2, ratio=6 - mbconv4: in=192, expanded=1152, out=288, stride=2, ratio=6 - mbconv5: in=288, expanded=1728, out=384, stride=1, ratio=6 - final: 384 -> 1408 So the kernel can be written specifically for these dimensions. Inside the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void tail_kernel(float* input, float* output, float* w_se1_1, float* w_se1_2, float* w_out1, float* b_out1, float* w_expand2, float* b_expand2, float* w_dw2, float* b_dw2, float* w_se2_1, float* w_se2_2, float* w_out2, float* b_out2, ... ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Shared memory for intermediate vectors __shared__ float s[...]; // max size needed is 1728 // Step 1: compute avgpool of input[b, :, :, :] // input shape: [B, 96, 112, 112] float v96[96]; // if using registers, but 96*4=384 bytes per thread. With 1024 threads, that's 384KB of registers - too much. // Better to use shared memory for all intermediate vectors. } ``` Actually, each thread block handles one sample. So shared memory only needs to hold vectors for one sample. Max vector size is 1728. `1728 * 4 = 7KB`. We need maybe 2 such buffers (ping-pong). `~14KB` of shared memory. That's very manageable (default 48KB). Number of threads per block: we can use 512 or 1024 threads to parallelize each matmul/elementwise op. But a simpler approach: within each block, we can have threads cooperate on matrix multiplications. However, since the vectors are small and the number of blocks is tiny (B=2), we want enough thread blocks to utilize the GPU. With only 2 blocks, we'd want each block to have many threads and high occupancy. 1024 threads per block is good. For each operation (e.g., matmul A[M,N] @ x[N] -> y[M]), we can have threads compute elements of y. Specifically, each thread computes `y[i] = sum_j A[i,j] * x[j] + b[i]` for some i. For M up to 1728 and 1024 threads, some threads compute 1 element, some 2. We can use a loop: ```cuda for (int i = tid; i < M; i += blockDim.x) { float sum = b[i]; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { sum += A[i*N + j] * x[j]; } y[i] = sum; } __syncthreads(); ``` This is simple and correct. The performance should be okay because: - A is in global memory (coalesced access since threads read contiguous rows? Actually if A is row-major [M,N], thread i reads A[i*N + j] for fixed i and varying j. That's contiguous in j for a single thread, but not coalesced across threads. Wait, if thread tid reads row `i = tid`, then for a fixed `j`, threads are reading `A[i*N + j]` which is strided by N. That is NOT coalesced! - Coalesced access: threads in a warp should access consecutive memory addresses. So we should have thread `tid` compute `y[tid]`, and when reading `A[tid*N + j]`, for a fixed `j`, consecutive threads read `A[tid*N + j]` which are N floats apart. Very bad. To fix coalescing, we should transpose the weights: store as [N, M] so that `A[j*M + i]` gives row i, col j. Then for fixed j, threads read `A[j*M + tid]` which is coalesced. Or we can just transpose the weight matrices on the host before passing to the kernel. Alternatively, we can use shared memory for the input vector x and have each thread compute one output element by reading the weight matrix in a coalesced way. If weights are in [M, N] row-major layout, thread i computing y[i] will read A[i*N+j] sequentially. This sequential access per thread is fine (memory coalescing is per warp, but a warp executing thread i will issue loads for different rows, which is strided and bad). Actually, CUDA memory coalescing: A warp of 32 threads executes the same instruction. If thread `lane` accesses `A[i*N + j]`, but `i` varies by thread, then for a fixed `j`, the accesses are `A[i0*N+j], A[i1*N+j], ...`. These are N*4 bytes apart. With N up to 1728 and 32 threads, the warp accesses 32 words scattered across memory with stride 1728. That's terrible. To fix this, we should transpose weights to [N, M]. Then `A[j*M + i]`. For fixed `j`, consecutive threads read consecutive memory: `A[j*M + i0], A[j*M + i0+1], ...`. This is perfectly coalesced. So in the Python code, before passing weights to the kernel, I'll transpose all linear weight matrices: `W_t = W.contiguous().t()`. And in the kernel, I'll read `W[j*M + i]`. Wait, but the kernel signature uses `float*`. And PyTorch tensors are passed by pointer. If I pass `W_t`, the pointer is to the transposed data. The kernel then does: ```cuda float sum = b[i]; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { sum += W_t[j*M + i] * x[j]; } ``` This is coalesced: for a fixed j, threads in a warp access `W_t[j*M + i0..i31]` which is contiguous. Great. What about reading x[j]? We can load x into shared memory to avoid redundant global reads and provide broadcast. Since N is up to 1728, `1728*4 = 7KB`. That's fine. We'll load x into `__shared__ float sx[2048]`. What about writing y? Each thread writes one element, which is coalesced if threads write to consecutive addresses. So the pattern inside the kernel can be: ```cuda __shared__ float buf1[2048]; __shared__ float buf2[2048]; float* x = buf1; float* y = buf2; // load x from global memory or compute avgpool directly into x ... // MatMul: y = W^T @ x + b, where W is MxN (row-major in PyTorch), // but we pass W_t of shape NxM (contiguous). // Actually, PyTorch linear: y = x @ W^T + b, where W is [out, in]. // So y_i = sum_j x_j * W_{i,j} + b_i. // If we pass W_data as W_{i,j} stored row-major [out, in], then W[i*in + j]. // But for coalescing, we want W_t where W_t[j*out + i] = W[i*in + j]. // Wait, if we transpose W, we get W^T of shape [in, out]. // In memory: W^T[j, i] is at W_t[j*out + i]. // Then y_i = sum_j x_j * W^T[j*out + i] + b_i. // For fixed j, threads read W_t[j*out + i] which is contiguous. Perfect. ``` So the kernel code will use transposed weights. Now let's plan the actual implementation: 1. For the CUDA kernel, I'll create a helper function: ```cuda __device__ void matvec_relu(const float* Wt, const float* b, const float* x, float* y, int M, int N) { // Wt is transposed weight of shape [N, M] in memory // computes y = W @ x + b, then relu for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < M; i += blockDim.x) { float sum = b[i]; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { sum += Wt[j * M + i] * x[j]; } y[i] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } } __device__ void matvec_sigmoid(...) { y[i] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-sum)); } __device__ void matvec(...) { y[i] = sum; } __device__ void elemwise_mul_add_relu(const float* w, const float* b, const float* x, float* y, int M) { for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < M; i += blockDim.x) { y[i] = fmaxf(x[i] * w[i] + b[i], 0.0f); } } ``` Wait, but `x` and `y` need to be in shared memory because all threads in the block access them. Wait, but there's a problem: each thread computing its own `y[i]` independently means each thread iterates over all j. So each weight matrix row is read by one thread. However, memory access is not fully coalesced across warps for the inner loop because... actually yes it is. For warp 0 (threads 0..31) computing i=0..31, for j=0 they read Wt[0*M + 0..31]. It is coalesced within each iteration, but consecutive iterations access far apart. This might cause bad cache locality. Because the inner loop is over j, and Wt is stored as [N, M], the access pattern is: for j=0: Wt[0*M + i] for j=1: Wt[1*M + i] ... for j=N-1: Wt[(N-1)*M + i] If N=1728 and M=1728, then for thread i, it accesses elements at i, i+M, i+2M, ..., i+(N-1)M. This is a column access pattern on the transposed matrix. If M is large (6912 bytes), then consecutive j accesses are 6912 bytes apart. The L2 cache line is 32-128 bytes, so each load brings in a small number of elements. With 1024 threads, each doing strided access, the cache might thrash. Wait. Stride = M elements = 6912 bytes. For a 32-byte cache line, that's 217 cache lines apart. So absolutely zero spatial locality between j iterations. The GPU will issue separate memory transactions for each j. Is this a problem? Memory throughput for strided but coalesced access: for a warp, at each j, it reads 128 bytes contiguously. So 1 memory transaction per j per warp. The total bytes read by a warp for all j is 128 * N. With N=1728, that's 221KB per warp. There are M/32 = 54 warps. Total memory traffic = 54 * 221KB = 11.9MB. Which is exactly the matrix size (1728*1728*4 = 11.9MB). So there's no redundant traffic. But can the latency be hidden? For each warp, the read at j=0 might miss L2. While waiting, the warp switches. Then j=1 read: again likely miss L2 because the stride is huge. However, the GPU memory subsystem can handle multiple concurrent memory requests. With 1024 threads = 32 warps, there might be enough warps to hide latency if they are at different j. But since all warps iterate j in lockstep, they will all request memory for j, then j+1, etc. So they all stall at the same time. We can improve this by processing the j dimension in tiles and using shared memory for the weights. For example, load a tile of Wt of size [M, TILE] into shared memory? But M=1728, and shared memory is 48KB. 1728 floats = 7KB. So we can actually load one column of Wt (all M elements for a fixed j) into shared memory. But that doesn't help because we need all j. Alternatively, we can transpose the weights differently: store as [M, N] in memory, and have threads read along N dimension contiguously. Let's reconsider. Original weight W is [M, N] (out, in). We want to compute `y[i] = sum_j W[i,j] * x[j]`. If we store W as [M, N] in memory (row-major), then for fixed i and varying j, thread i reads W[i*N + j] sequentially. This is very good for a single thread (coalesced within the thread? Actually a single thread reading sequentially is good for caches). But across threads: for fixed j, thread i reads W[i*N + j]. These are N elements apart. Not coalesced. The two choices: 1. Transposed W_t [N, M]: coalesced across threads for fixed j, but strided across j for a single thread. 2. Original W [M, N]: coalesced across threads for fixed j? No. Strided across threads for fixed j. Wait, I made a mistake. Let's think about coalescing again. In choice 1 (W_t [N, M]): `addr = W_t + j*M + i` For fixed j, as i varies by thread, addresses are consecutive. Coalesced across threads. For fixed i (single thread), as j varies, addresses are `W_t + j*M + i`. Stride M. In choice 2 (W [M, N]): `addr = W + i*N + j` For fixed j, as i varies, addresses are `W + i*N + j`. Stride N. Not coalesced. For fixed i, as j varies, addresses are consecutive. Good for a single thread. So choice 1 is better for coalescing across threads. The strided access across j for a single thread is unavoidable unless we reorder the loop. We can improve cache locality by accessing j in a blocked/tiled fashion and loading blocks of x into shared memory? Actually, x is small enough to fit in shared memory. But for W_t, we could process chunks of j where consecutive j are close in memory? No, in W_t [N,M], j changes the major dimension. W_t[j*M + i] and W_t[(j+1)*M + i] are M elements apart. So no locality. But what if we swap the loops? Instead of each thread computing one output element by looping over the full input, we can have all threads help compute partial sums for all outputs and accumulate in shared memory. Specifically, we can process the input in tiles. Load a tile of x into shared memory. Then for each output i, we accumulate the dot product. But accumulating across threads requires reduction. Here's a better approach for small GEMV on GPU: Use one block per output vector. Each block has, say, 256 threads. The block computes `y = W @ x`. Divide the input vector x into chunks of 256. Each thread loads one x element into shared memory. Then we iterate over output elements i. For each i, each thread computes `partial = W[i, j] * x[j]` for its j, then a block reduction (warp shuffle or shared memory) sums the partials. This approach has each thread read W[i,j] for consecutive j. Since j is the thread index, W[i,j] for consecutive threads are consecutive in memory IF W is stored as [M, N] (row-major). Wait! W[i,j] = W + i*N + j. For fixed i, as j varies (j=threadIdx.x), addresses are consecutive! This is perfectly coalesced! And for x[j], consecutive threads load consecutive j into shared memory, also coalesced. So this is the ideal pattern! Algorithm: ```cuda __shared__ float x_tile[THREADS]; __shared__ float partials[THREADS]; // load x into shared memory (coalesced) for (int j_base = 0; j_base < N; j_base += THREADS) { int j = j_base + threadIdx.x; if (j < N) x_tile[threadIdx.x] = x[j]; __syncthreads(); // Now for each output i for (int i = blockStart; i < blockEnd; ++i) { float val = (j < N) ? W[i*N + j] * x_tile[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; // block reduction sum of val __syncthreads(); partials[threadIdx.x] = val; __syncthreads(); // tree reduction or warp shuffle for (int s = THREADS/2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) partials[threadIdx.x] += partials[threadIdx.x + s]; __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { y[i] = partials[0] + b[i]; // +bias } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, but this has `__syncthreads()` inside the i loop, which is fine. However, the inner loop over i means that for each i, the block loads W[i*N + j_base..j_base+THREADS-1]. For a warp, loading W[i*N + j_base + lane] is perfectly coalesced (consecutive addresses). Great! The issue: for each i, we do a block reduction. With M up to 1728, N up to 1728, and THREADS=256: - Number of tiles for x: ceil(1728/256) = 7. - For each output i (1728 outputs), we do 7 tiles * reduction(256). - Total reductions: 1728 * 7 = ~12K block reductions per matmul. - Each reduction has ~8 steps of shuffle/syncthreads. - This might be slow due to syncthreads overhead. Another approach: assign each thread to one output i. Then loop over j in tiles. For each j tile, load x into shm. Then each thread computes its dot product partial sum by reading W[i, j] for j in tile. But thread 0 reads W[0, j], thread 1 reads W[1, j], etc. These are N elements apart. Not coalesced. We need to balance coalescing and reduction overhead. A third approach: transpose W to [N, M]. Each thread assigned to one output i. Loop j over tiles. Load W_t[j_start + tid, i]? No. Actually, if W is stored as [N, M] (column-major for the original W), then W_t[j, i] = original W[i, j]. If we process j in tiles of THREADS, and for each tile, have the threads load W_t[j_start + tid, i_start..i_start+WARP-1] somehow... this gets complicated. Let's go back to the simplest approach: each thread computes one or more output elements by looping over j. With W_t [N,M]: ```cuda for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < M; i += blockDim.x) { float sum = b[i]; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { sum += W_t[j*M + i] * x[j]; } y[i] = sum; } ``` With blockDim=1024, and M=1728, we have about 1.7 elements per thread. Memory access: for each j, all active threads in a warp read W_t[j*M + tid], which is coalesced. After reading, the warp proceeds to j+1, reading W_t[(j+1)*M + tid]. The distance between W_t[j*M] and W_t[(j+1)*M] is M floats = ~7KB. For 32 warps running concurrently, they might be at different stages of j? Actually in lockstep, all warps increments j together. With L2 cache capacity, these might not all fit. L2 cache on A100 is 40MB. The weights for one large matmul are 12MB. So W_t might stay in L2 for the duration of the matmul. Actually, 12MB is big but less than L2. So as we iterate j, we load W_t[j*M + 0..1023] into L2. Then next j, load W_t[(j+1)*M + 0..1023]. Since we're scanning the entire W_t matrix, and 12MB < L2, the whole matrix might reside in L2 and be served from L2, not DRAM. This is great! Let's estimate more carefully: Total weights for all mats: ~25MB (most are small, only a few large ones). 25MB < L2 cache (40MB). So all weights can fit in L2 simultaneously! This is crucial. Shared memory buffer: I'll use two buffers, `buf1` and `buf2`, large enough for 2048 floats. ```cuda #define MAX_DIM 2048 __shared__ float s_buf1[MAX_DIM]; __shared__ float s_buf2[MAX_DIM]; ``` Helper device functions: ```cuda __device__ void load_to_shared(const float* gmem, float* shm, int N, int tid, int blockSize) { for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockSize) { shm[i] = gmem[i]; } __syncthreads(); } __device__ void linear_relu(const float* Wt, const float* b, const float* x, float* y, int M, int N, int tid, int blockSize) { // Wt shape [N, M] in memory for (int i = tid; i < M; i += blockSize) { float sum = b[i]; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { sum += Wt[j * M + i] * x[j]; } y[i] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } __syncthreads(); } __device__ void linear_sigmoid(const float* Wt, const float* b, const float* x, float* y, int M, int N, int tid, int blockSize) { for (int i = tid; i < M; i += blockSize) { float sum = b[i]; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { sum += Wt[j * M + i] * x[j]; } y[i] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-sum)); } __syncthreads(); } __device__ void linear(const float* Wt, const float* b, const float* x, float* y, int M, int N, int tid, int blockSize) { for (int i = tid; i < M; i += blockSize) { float sum = b[i]; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { sum += Wt[j * M + i] * x[j]; } y[i] = sum; } __syncthreads(); } __device__ void elemwise_mul_add_relu(const float* w, const float* b, const float* x, float* y, int M, int tid, int blockSize) { for (int i = tid; i < M; i += blockSize) { y[i] = fmaxf(x[i] * w[i] + b[i], 0.0f); } __syncthreads(); } ``` Tail kernel: ```cuda __global__ void efficientnet_tail_kernel( const float* input, // [B, 96, 112, 112] float* output, // [B, 1000] int B, // mbconv1 tail const float* w_se1_1, const float* w_se1_2, const float* w_out1, const float* b_out1, // mbconv2 const float* w_expand2, const float* b_expand2, const float* w_dw2, const float* b_dw2, const float* w_se2_1, const float* w_se2_2, const float* w_out2, const float* b_out2, // mbconv3 const float* w_expand3, const float* b_expand3, const float* w_dw3, const float* b_dw3, const float* w_se3_1, const float* w_se3_2, const float* w_out3, const float* b_out3, // mbconv4 const float* w_expand4, const float* b_expand4, const float* w_dw4, const float* b_dw4, const float* w_se4_1, const float* w_se4_2, const float* w_out4, const float* b_out4, // mbconv5 const float* w_expand5, const float* b_expand5, const float* w_dw5, const float* b_dw5, const float* w_se5_1, const float* w_se5_2, const float* w_out5, const float* b_out5, // final + fc const float* w_final, const float* b_final, const float* w_fc, const float* b_fc) { int b_idx = blockIdx.x; s += blockSize) { sum += input[offset + c * spatial + s]; } // reduction within block for this channel // We can use shared memory reduction for sum // But simpler: atomicAdd to x[c] atomicAdd(&x[c], sum); For 1024 threads, 384KB register pressure - too much. // Better: have each warp compute one channel at a time. // Or: each thread computes partial sum for one channel over its spatial subset, then atomicAdd. // There are 96 channels. A much simpler way: ```cuda int spatial = 112 * 112; int offset = b_idx * 96 * spatial; for (int c = tid; c < 96; c += blockSize) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s < spatial; ++s) { sum += input[offset + c * spatial + s]; } x[c] = sum / spatial; } __syncthreads(); ``` This is coalesced: thread c reads input[offset + c*spatial + s] for fixed c and varying s. Since spatial is large and s is sequential, this is perfectly sequential for a thread. Not coalesced across threads, but since each thread reads a full channel worth of data sequentially, it'll be streamed efficiently from global memory. And L2 can handle it. With blockSize=1024, only 96 threads do work. The other 928 threads are idle. Is that okay? Yes, because avgpool is only 1.2M reads. At 1TB/s, that's 1.2us. But atomicAdd on shared memory is fast. ```cuda for (int c = 0; c < 96; ++c) { float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int s = tid; s < spatial; s += blockSize) { local_sum += input[offset + c * spatial + s]; } atomicAdd(&x[c], local_sum); } __syncthreads(); if (tid < 96) x[tid] /= spatial; __syncthreads(); ``` Here, all 1024 threads participate. Thread tid reads input[offset + c*spatial + tid], which for fixed c, threads read consecutive addresses. Coalesced! Much better. And atomicAdd to shared memory is very fast (essentially L1 latency). Since all threads in a warp will atomicAdd to the same x[c], there will be address conflicts. But shared memory can handle some bank conflicts. Wait, I need to be careful about `elemwise_mul_add_relu`. The operation is: `y_i = relu(x_i * w_i + b_i)`. This assumes x and y are both length M. In step 2 of mbconv2, v3 is [576], and we apply element-wise multiply with w_dw2[576], add b_dw2[576]. So yes, same dimension. Then after final linear_relu: `v27 = relu(linear(v26, W_final, b_final))` where v26 is [384], W_final is [1408, 384], so v27 is [1408]. Then FC: `output = linear(v27, W_fc, b_fc)` where W_fc is [1000, 1408]. All dimensions match. What about `b_fc`? `nn.Linear` in PyTorch computes `x @ W^T + b`, where W is [out, in] and b is [out]. Yes. Now, for the weight transpose: In the kernel, for `linear`, I read `Wt[j*M + i]`. This corresponds to `W[i, j]`. So `Wt` should be the transpose of the PyTorch weight `W` which has shape `[out, in] = [M, N]`. `W_t = W.t().contiguous()` gives shape `[N, M]`. Then `W_t[j, i] == W[i, j]`. In the kernel: `Wt[j*M + i]` is correct. Great. One concern: the `expf` in sigmoid. `sigmoid(x) = 1 / (1 + exp(-x))`. For large positive x, `exp(-x)` underflows to 0, and it's fine. For large negative x, `exp(-x)` overflows to inf. We should guard against this. ```cuda float sigmoid(float x) { if (x >= 0) { float z = expf(-x); return 1.0f / (1.0f + z); } else { float z = expf(x); return z / (1.0f + z); } } ``` This is numerically stable. But for our range, overflow might not happen. Still, good to include. Similarly for ReLU: `fmaxf(sum, 0.0f)`. Now for writing the actual kernel code. For the Python side: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_sources = r''' __device__ inline float Wt, const float* b, const float* x, float* y, int M, int N, int tid, int bsz) { for (int i = tid; i < M; i += bsz) { float acc = b[i]; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += Wt[j * M + i] * x[j]; } y[i] This might have issues with `__syncthreads()` inside `matmul` because `output` is global memory, not shared memory. The `__syncthreads()` is fine because it's a block-level sync. The function still works. But the pointer passed as `y` should be global memory. The `__syncthreads()` at the end of `matmul` just ensures all threads in the block are done before moving to the next operation. Since there's no next operation after FC, it's fine. However, in `matmul`, the `y` pointer could point to global memory, but all threads in the block write to it. That's okay. The intermediate buffers `buf1` and `buf2` are shared memory. This is fine because `__shared__` is a storage qualifier that applies to the variable declaration, not the pointer type. The output tensor is `[B, 1000]`. We write directly to global memory. No `__syncthreads()` needed after, but it's fine. Let's now write the C++ wrapper and Python side. In C++: ```cpp void forward_tail( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor output, // se1 torch::Tensor se1_w, torch::Tensor se1_b, torch::Tensor se2_w, torch::Tensor se2_b, torch::Tensor out1_w, torch::Tensor out1_b, // mbconv2 torch::Tensor expand2_w, torch::Tensor expand2_b, torch::Tensor dw2_w, torch::Tensor dw2_b, torch::Tensor se2_1_w, torch::Tensor se2_1_b, torch::Tensor se2_2_w, torch::Tensor se2_2_b, torch::Tensor out2_w, torch::Tensor out2_b, // mbconv3 torch::Tensor expand3_w, torch::Tensor expand3_b, torch::Tensor dw3_w, torch::Tensor dw3_b, torch::Tensor se3_1_w, torch::Tensor se3_1_b, torch::Tensor se3_2_w, torch::Tensor se3_2_b, torch::Tensor out3_w, torch::Tensor out3_b, // mbconv4 torch::Tensor expand4_w, torch::Tensor expand4_b, torch::Tensor dw4_w, torch::Tensor dw4_b, torch::Tensor se4_1_w, torch::Tensor se4_1_b, torch::Tensor se4_2_w, torch::Tensor se4_2_b, torch::Tensor out4_w, torch::Tensor out4_b, // mbconv5 torch::Tensor expand5_w, torch::Tensor expand5_b, torch::Tensor dw5_w, torch::Tensor dw5_b, torch::Tensor se5_1_w, torch::Tensor se5_1_b, torch::Tensor se5_2_w, torch::Tensor se5_2_b, torch::Tensor out5_w, torch::Tensor out5_b, // final and fc torch::Tensor final_w, torch::Tensor final_b, torch::Tensor fc_w, torch::Tensor fc_b) { int B = input.size(0); tail_kernel<<<B, 1024>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, se1_w.data_ptr<float>(), se1_b.data_ptr<float>(), se2_w.data_ptr<float>(), se2_b.data_ptr<float>(), out1_w.data_ptr<float>(), out1_b.data_ptr<float>(), expand2_w.data_ptr<float>(), expand2_b.data_ptr<float>(), dw2_w.data_ptr<float>(), dw2_b.data_ptr<float>(), se2_1_w.data_ptr<float>(), se2_1_b.data_ptr<float>(), se2_2_w.data_ptr<float>(), se2_2_b.data_ptr<float>(), out2_w.data_ptr<float>(), out2_b.data_ptr<float>(), expand3_w.data_ptr<float>(), expand3_b.data_ptr<float>(), dw3_w.data_ptr<float>(), dw3_b.data_ptr<float>(), se3_1_w.data_ptr<float>(), se3_1_b.data_ptr<float>(), se3_2_w.data_ptr<float>(), se3_2_b.data_ptr<float>(), out3_w.data_ptr<float>(), out3_b.data_ptr<float>(), expand4_w.data_ptr<float>(), expand4_b.data_ptr<float>(), dw4_w.data_ptr<float>(), dw4_b.data_ptr<float>(), se4_1_w.data_ptr<float>(), se4_1_b.data_ptr<float>(), se4_2_w.data_ptr<float>(), se4_2_b.data_ptr<float>(), out4_w.data_ptr<float>(), out4_b.data_ptr<float>(), expand5_w.data_ptr<float>(), expand5_b.data_ptr<float>(), dw5_w.data_ptr<float>(), dw5_b.data_ptr<float>(), se5_1_w.data_ptr<float>(), se5_1_b.data_ptr<float>(), se5_2_w.data_ptr<float>(), se5_2_b.data_ptr<float>(), out5_w.data_ptr<float>(), out5_b.data_ptr<float>(), final_w.data_ptr<float>(), final_b.data_ptr<float>(), fc_w.data_ptr<float>(), fc_b.data_ptr<float>()); } ``` This is extremely long. But it's generated code; I can write it. In Python, the `forward` method: ```python def forward(self, x): x = F.relu(self.conv1(x)) x = F.relu(self.expand1(x)) x = F.relu(self.dw1(x)) # x is [B, 96, 112, 112] out = torch.empty(x.size(0), 1000, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) self.cuda_module.forward_tail( x, out, self.se1_w, self.se1_b, ... ) return out ``` Wait, but the first three convs: I need to pre-fuse them. `self.conv1` = fused conv1 (bias=True) `self.expand1` = fused expand conv in mbconv1 (bias=True) `self.dw1` = fused depthwise conv in mbconv1 (bias=True) How to get these fused weights: 1. Instantiate `Model` reference. 2. For each conv+BN pair, compute fused weight and bias. 3. Create new `nn.Conv2d` layers with `bias=True` and copy fused weights/biases. 4. For depthwise conv, weight shape is `[out, 1, 3, 3]`. Fused weight: `weight * (gamma/std).view(-1,1,1,1)`. Bias: `beta - mean * gamma/std`. Code for Python fusion: ```python def fuse_conv_bn(conv, bn): with torch.no_grad(): w = conv.weight.data mean = bn.running_mean var = bn.running_var gamma = bn.weight beta = bn.bias eps = bn.eps std = torch.sqrt(var + eps) w_new = w * (gamma / std).view(-1, 1, 1, 1) b_new = beta - mean * gamma / std return w_new, b_new ``` For mbconv1 layers: `ref = Model(num_classes)` Then extract `ref.mbconv1[0]` (expand conv), `ref.mbconv1[1]` (expand bn), etc. But my `_fuse_mbconv` earlier just replaced Conv+BN pairs. Actually, I should extract the fused weights directly instead of creating new nn.Sequential. In ModelNew.__init__: ```python ref = Model(num_classes) ref.eval() # conv1 w1, b1 = fuse_conv_bn(ref.conv1, ref.bn1) self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(3, 32, kernel_size=3, stride=2, padding=1, bias=True) self.conv1.weight.data.copy_(w1) self.conv1.bias.data.copy_(b1) # mbconv1 # ref.mbconv1 is Sequential mb1 = ref.mbconv1 # expand conv+bn w_exp1, b_exp1 = fuse_conv_bn(mb1[0], mb1[1]) self.expand1 = nn.Conv2d(32, 96, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=True) self.expand1.weight.data.copy_(w_exp1) self.expand1.bias.data.copy_(b_exp1) # depthwise conv+bn w_dw1, b_dw1 = fuse_conv_bn(mb1[3], mb1[4]) self.dw1 = nn.Conv2d(96, 96, kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=1, groups=96, bias=True) self.dw1.weight.data.copy_(w_dw1) self.dw1.bias.data.copy_(b_dw1) # Now extract SE weights # mb1[6] is AvgPool, mb1[7] is Conv2d(96,24,1x1,bias=False) se1_w = mb1[7].weight.data.view(24, 96).t().contiguous() se1_b = torch.zeros(24, device=se1_w.device, dtype=se1_w.dtype) # mb1[9] is Conv2d(24,96,1x1,bias=False) se2_w = mb1[9].weight.data.view(96, 24).t().contiguous() se2_b = torch.zeros(96, device=se2_w.device, dtype=se2_w.dtype) # mb1[11] is Conv2d(96,96,1x1,bias=False) out1_w, out1_b = fuse_conv_bn(mb1[11], mb1[12]) out1_w = out1_w.view(96, 96).t().contiguous() # mbconv2 mb2 = ref.mbconv2 w_exp2, b_exp2 = fuse_conv_bn(mb2[0], mb2[1]) self.expand2_w = w_exp2.view(576, 96).t().contiguous() # But wait, we don't use expand2 as nn.Conv2d! We pass it to kernel. ... ``` Wait, if we pass expand2_w to the kernel, we need it to be a parameter or buffer so it moves with the model. ```python self.register_buffer('expand2_w', w_exp2.view(576,96).t().contiguous()) self.register_buffer('expand2_b', b_exp2) ``` Similarly for depthwise: we need to fuse the center weight and BN. ```python # mb2[3] is depthwise conv, mb2[4] is BN w_dw2_orig = mb2[3].weight.data # [576,1,3,3] mean = mb2[4].running_mean var = mb2[4].running_var gamma = mb2[4].weight beta = mb2[4].bias eps = mb2[4].eps std = torch.sqrt(var + eps) w_dw2 = w_dw2_orig[:, 0, 1, 1] * (gamma / std) b_dw2 = beta - mean * gamma / std self.register_buffer('dw2_w', w_dw2) self.register_buffer('dw2_b', b_dw2) ``` For mbconv2 SE layers: ```python se2_1_w = mb2[7].weight.data.view(144, 576).t().contiguous() se2_1_b = torch.zeros_like(se2_1_w[:,0]) # or just size [144] # Actually, torch.zeros(144, device=..., dtype=...) ... ``` For mbconv2 output: ```python out2_w, out2_b = fuse_conv_bn(mb2[11], mb2[12]) out2_w = out2_w.view(144, 576).t().contiguous() ``` And so on for mbconv3, mbconv4, mbconv5. For conv_final: ```python w_final, b_final = fuse_conv_bn(ref.conv_final, ref.bn_final) final_w = w_final.view(1408, 384).t().contiguous() ``` For fc: ```python fc_w = ref.fc.weight.data.t().contiguous() fc_b = ref.fc.bias.data ``` This is a lot of code but very mechanical. I need to be careful about which layer index corresponds to what in the fused sequential. My `_fuse_mbconv` changes the sequential. It's easier to just work with the original `ref.mbconv1` sequential and pick out the raw convs and BNs by their types, not by index. Robust extraction: ```python def get_conv_bn_relu_weights(seq): # seq is a nn.Sequential from _make_mbconv_block # Returns list of (conv_weight, conv_bias_or_None, has_bn) ... pass ``` Actually, let's just index by layer type. We know exactly the architecture. For any mbconv block: - If expand_ratio != 1: - layers[0]: Conv2d - layers[1]: BN - layers[2]: ReLU - layers[3]: Depthwise Conv2d - layers[4]: BN - layers[5]: ReLU - layers[6]: AvgPool - layers[7]: SE Conv2d (bias=False) - layers[8]: ReLU - layers[9]: SE Conv2d (bias=False) - layers[10]: Sigmoid - layers[11]: Output Conv2d (bias=False) - layers[12]: BN - If expand_ratio == 1: - layers[0]: Depthwise Conv2d - layers[1]: BN - layers[2]: ReLU - layers[3]: AvgPool - layers[4]: SE Conv2d - layers[5]: ReLU - layers[6]: SE Conv2d - layers[7]: Sigmoid - layers[8]: Output Conv2d - layers[9]: BN For mbconv1, ratio=3, so there are 13 layers. For mbconv2-5, ratio=6, so there are 13 layers. For mbconv5, ratio=6, 13 layers. Wait, in `_make_mbconv_block`: ```python if expand_ratio != 1: layers.append(nn.Conv2d(in_channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False)) layers.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(expanded_channels)) layers.append(nn.ReLU(inplace=True)) layers.append(nn.Conv2d(expanded_channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size=3, stride=stride, padding=1, groups=expanded_channels, bias=False)) layers.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(expanded_channels)) layers.append(nn.ReLU(inplace=True)) layers.append(nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1, 1))) layers.append(nn.Conv2d(expanded_channels, expanded_channels // 4, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False)) layers.append(nn.ReLU(inplace=True)) layers.append(nn.Conv2d(expanded_channels // 4, expanded_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False)) layers.append(nn.Sigmoid()) layers.append(nn.Conv2d(expanded_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False)) layers.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels)) ``` So if expand_ratio != 1, total layers = 3 + 10 = 13. If expand_ratio == 1, total = 10. All blocks in this model have expand_ratio != 1 (mbconv1 ratio=3, others 6). So all have 13 layers. Let's verify indexes: 0: expand conv 1: expand bn 2: relu 3: depthwise conv 4: depthwise bn 5: relu 6: avgpool 7: se_conv1 8: relu 9: se_conv2 10: sigmoid 11: output conv 12: output bn Great. Now for Python weight extraction: ```python def extract_mbconv_weights(mbconv_seq): w_expand, b_expand = fuse_conv_bn(mbconv_seq[0], mbconv_seq[1]) w_dw, b_dw = fuse_conv_bn(mbconv_seq[3], mbconv_seq[4]) # se1 w_se1 = mbconv_seq[7].weight.data.squeeze().t().contiguous() # [expanded//4, expanded] -> [expanded, expanded//4] b_se1 = torch.zeros(mbconv_seq[7].out_channels, device=w_se1.device, dtype=w_se1.dtype) # se2 w_se2 = mbconv_seq[9].weight.data.squeeze().t().contiguous() b_se2 = torch.zeros(mbconv_seq[9].out_channels, device=w_se2.device, dtype=w_se2.dtype) # out w_out, b_out = fuse_conv_bn(mbconv_seq[11], mbconv_seq[12]) w_out = w_out.squeeze().t().contiguous() return (w_expand, b_expand, w_dw, b_dw, w_se1, b_se1, w_se2, b_se2, w_out, b_out) ``` Wait, `squeeze()` on Conv2d weight of shape `[out, in, 1, 1]` gives `[out, in]`. Yes. Then `t()` gives `[in, out]`. This is correct for the kernel (Wt). `contiguous()` ensures row-major storage. For depthwise center weight: ```python w_dw_orig = mbconv_seq[3].weight.data w_dw_center = w_dw_orig[:, 0, 1, 1] # shape [expanded_channels] ``` Then after fusing with BN: ```python std = torch.sqrt(mbconv_seq[4].running_var + mbconv_seq[4].eps) w_dw_fused = w_dw_center * (mbconv_seq[4].weight / std) b_dw_fused = mbconv_seq[4].bias - mbconv_seq[4].running_mean * mbconv_seq[4].weight / std ``` Wait, my `fuse_conv_bn` already does this for the full conv weight. For depthwise, `fuse_conv_bn` returns `w_new` of shape `[expanded, 1, 3, 3]`. Then `w_new[:,0,1,1]` is the center weight. `b_new` is the fused bias. So: ```python w_dw_full, b_dw_full = fuse_conv_bn(mbconv_seq[3], mbconv_seq[4]) w_dw = w_dw_full[:, 0, 1, 1].contiguous() b_dw = b_dw_full ``` This is cleaner. For expand and output conv, `fuse_conv_bn` gives `w_new` of shape `[out, in, 1, 1]`. We can do: ```python w_expand = w_expand_new.view(out_channels, in_channels).t().contiguous() ``` But we need to know `in_channels` and `out_channels`. We can get them from the conv layer: `mbconv_seq[0].in_channels`, `mbconv_seq[0].out_channels`. Wait, expand conv: `in_channels` -> `expanded_channels`. But `mbconv_seq[0]` is not defined if expand_ratio==1. But in our case all blocks have expand. For mbconv1: mbconv_seq[0] is Conv2d(32, 96, 1x1). mbconv_seq[3] is Conv2d(96, 96, 3x3, g=96). mbconv_seq[11] is Conv2d(96, 96, 1x1). For mbconv2: mbconv_seq[0] is Conv2d(96, 576, 1x1). mbconv_seq[3] is Conv2d(576, 576, 3x3, g=576). mbconv_seq[11] is Conv2d(576, 144, 1x1). So the `out_channels` of mbconv_seq[11] is the block output. I'll write extraction code carefully. Now, after setting up all weights, the forward pass is: ```python def forward(self, x): x = F.relu(self.conv1(x)) x = F.relu(self.expand1(x)) x = F.relu(self.dw1(x)) out = torch.empty(x.size(0), 1000, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) self.cuda_module.forward_tail(x, out, self.se1_w, self.se1_b, self.se2_w, self.se2_b, self.out1_w, self.out1_b, self.expand2_w, self.expand2_b, self.dw2_w, self.dw2_b, self.se2_1_w, self.se2_1_b, self.se2_2_w, self.se2_2_b, self.out2_w, self.out2_b, self.expand3_w, self.expand3_b, self.dw3_w, self.dw3_b, self.se3_1_w, self.se3_1_b, self.se3_2_w, self.se3_2_b, self.out3_w, self.out3_b, self.expand4_w, self.expand4_b, self.dw4_w, self.dw4_b, self.se4_1_w, self.se4_1_b, self.se4_2_w, self.se4_2_b, self.out4_w, self.out4_b, self.expand5_w, self.expand5_b, self.dw5_w, self.dw5_b, self.se5_1_w, self.se5_1_b, self.se5_2_w, self.se5_2_b, self.out5_w, self.out5_b, self.final_w, self.final_b, self.fc_w, self.fc_b) return out ``` This is long but workable. One issue: `load_inline` with this many functions? We only have ONE function `forward_tail` in `functions=[...]`. The arguments are just tensors. It's fine. Wait, I realize I also need to handle the `dw1` depthwise conv on 112x112. I plan to use `self.dw1` (standard nn.Conv2d) for this. But `fuse_conv_bn` on a depthwise conv gives a `nn.Conv2d` with bias. PyTorch's `F.conv2d` with groups will handle it correctly. For `conv1` and `expand1`: standard `nn.Conv2d` with bias. Great. Potential correctness issues: 1. Floating point order: the custom kernel computes dot products in a different order than cuBLAS/cuDNN (j loop sequentially). This might cause small numerical differences, but fp32 atol=1e-4 should be fine. 2. `expf` vs `exp` vs stable sigmoid. The stable sigmoid should match PyTorch well. 3. Bias shapes: need to make sure 1D bias vectors are contiguous. 4. `view` vs `squeeze`: `weight.data.view(out, in)` works for [out, in, 1, 1]. `squeeze()` also works. Wait, if `fuse_conv_bn` returns weight shape `[C_out, C_in, 1, 1]`, then `view(C_out, C_in)` is valid because the total number of elements is C_out * C_in. Also, for depthwise conv weight `[C, 1, 3, 3]`, `view(C, 3, 3)` is valid, and `[:, 0, 1, 1]` gets the center element. One more edge case: the reference model uses `inplace=True` ReLU. This doesn't affect the output values, just memory usage. Our fused version should match numerically. Also, `nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1,1))` computes the mean over all spatial locations. Our avgpool divides by `112*112`. This matches PyTorch's implementation (which divides by the number of elements, which is H*W). Now, will the custom kernel be significantly faster? Let's estimate reference time for tail: - mbconv1: avgpool(112x112, 96 ch) -> 1 kernel se1 (conv1x1 96->24) -> 1 kernel relu -> 1 kernel se2 (conv1x1 24->96) -> 1 kernel sigmoid -> 1 kernel out (conv1x1 96->96) -> 1 kernel bn -> 1 kernel = 7 kernels - mbconv2-5: similar, ~7 kernels each = 28 kernels - final: conv1x1 384->1408 -> 1 kernel bn -> 1 kernel relu -> 1 kernel - avgpool -> 1 kernel - flatten -> no kernel - fc (linear) -> 1 kernel Total tail kernels in reference: ~40 kernel launches. Plus the first 3 convs/bn/relu in the head: ~6 kernels (conv1, bn, relu, expand, bn, relu, dw, bn, relu -> actually 9 kernels). Total ~50 kernel launches. At ~5-10us per launch, that's ~500us. Custom approach: - conv1 (cuDNN): 1 kernel - expand1 (cuDNN): 1 kernel - dw1 (cuDNN): 1 kernel - tail custom kernel: 1 kernel Total: 4 kernels. ~40us launch overhead. The actual compute is the same or better fused. The custom kernel for the tail might take ~50-100us. So total time: ~100-200us. This should be significantly faster than 500us. Maybe 2-5x speedup. Is it possible the reference PyTorch is already fusing some ops via cuDNN? For example, conv+bias+relu is fused automatically if using cudnn. But here, the reference convs have `bias=False`, so cuDNN sees conv followed by BN and ReLU. Modern PyTorch + cuDNN might fuse conv+bn+relu if using `torch.backends.cudnn.benchmark` or if the graph is captured. But in eager mode with `bias=False`, cuDNN might still fuse conv+bias+relu if we manually add bias. Wait, the reference doesn't have bias. So cuDNN might launch separate kernels for conv, then PyTorch launches BN, then ReLU. Or, PyTorch's ATen might fuse BN+ReLU into one kernel, but conv is separate. So the reference probably has ~3 kernels per conv block: conv, (bn+relu fused by ATen maybe?), plus the ones after. For tiny 1x1 convs, cuDNN might fall back to a generic implementation or call cuBLAS. Either way, kernel launch overhead dominates. I think the custom kernel approach will win. Let me write the complete code. I'll be very careful with the string escaping for `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`. I'll also check that I use `r'''...'''` raw strings for the CUDA code to avoid backslash issues. Actually, one concern: when I do `w.view(C_out, C_in).t()` on a tensor that might not be contiguous, `.t()` might not make it contiguous. So I should do `.contiguous()`. `w.squeeze().t().contiguous()` is safe. One more thing: `mbconv_seq[7].out_channels` might be `expanded_channels // 4`. For mbconv1: 96//4=24. Good. But `mbconv_seq[7].weight.data.squeeze().t().contiguous()` - `squeeze()` removes all dimensions of size 1. For weight `[24, 96, 1, 1]`, squeeze gives `[24, 96]`. Then `.t()` gives `[96, 24]`. Good. For mbconv_seq[9] weight `[96, 24, 1, 1]`, squeeze gives `[96, 24]`. `.t()` gives `[24, 96]`. Good. For mbconv_seq[11] weight `[96, 96, 1, 1]`, squeeze gives `[96, 96]`. `.t()` gives `[96, 96]`. Good. Let's write all the extraction code. ```python import math def fuse_conv_bn_weights(conv, bn): with torch.no_grad(): w = conv.weight.data.clone() mean = bn.running_mean var = bn.running_var gamma = bn.weight.data beta = bn.bias.data eps = bn.eps std = torch.sqrt(var + eps) w_new = w * (gamma / std).view(-1, 1, 1, 1) b_new = beta - mean * gamma / std return w_new, b_new ``` And in `__init__`: ```python ref = Model(num_classes) ref.eval() w, b = fuse_conv_bn_weights(ref.conv1, ref.bn1) self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(3, 32, kernel_size=3, stride=2, padding=1, bias=True) self.conv1.weight.copy_(w) self.conv1.bias.copy_(b) mb1 = ref.mbconv1 w, b = fuse_conv_bn_weights(mb1[0], mb1[1]) self.expand1 = nn.Conv2d(32, 96, kernel_size=1, stride=1, bias=True) self.expand1.weight.copy_(w) self.expand1.bias.copy_(b) w, b = fuse_conv_bn_weights(mb1[3], mb1[4]) self.dw1 = nn.Conv2d(96, 96, kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=1, groups=96, bias=True) self.dw1.weight.copy_(w) self.dw1.bias.copy_(b) # Actually, looking at how `load_inline` is typically used: ```python mod = load_inline( name='...', cpp_sources='void my_func(torch::Tensor x) { ... }', cuda_sources='__global__ void kernel(...) { ... }', functions=['my_func'], ) ``` In this pattern, `my_func` is defined in `cpp_sources`. And the `cuda_sources` contains the `__global__` kernel. There is no issue with declarations because the `cpp_sources` includes the host wrapper that calls the kernel. So the standard pattern is: - `cpp_sources` contains the C++ host functions that are exposed to Python, and these functions call the CUDA kernels. - `cuda_sources` contains the `__global__` kernels and perhaps helper device functions. But in this pattern, the host functions in `cpp_sources` need to call the kernels, which means they need the kernel declarations. Usually, the `__global__` kernels can be forward-declared in `cpp_sources`, or the `cpp_sources` can include a header that declares them. But in `load_inline`, both sources are compiled. `cpp_sources` is compiled with the C++ compiler (g++), which does not allow `<<< >>>`. But it can contain the kernel forward declaration (with `extern "C"` or just without body) because a forward declaration of a `__global__` function is just a regular function declaration from the C++ compiler's perspective! It's only the definition (with `__global__`) that needs nvcc, and the call site with `<<< >>>` that needs nvcc. Ah, so the issue is: the C++ compiler cannot parse the `<<< >>>` syntax. But it CAN parse the kernel call if we wrap it in a `.cu` file (which is compiled by nvcc). So the host function that issues the kernel call must be in a `.cu` file. Therefore, the standard pattern with `load_inline` is: - Define the host launcher (with `<<< >>>`) in `cuda_sources`. - In `cpp_sources`, declare the host launcher (just a regular function declaration). - Have the Python-exposed function in `cpp_sources` call the host launcher. The depthwise conv on 1x1 input with 3x3 kernel, stride=2, padding=1: - input size 1x1 -> padded to 3x3 - kernel 3x3, stride=2 -> output size floor((3-3)/2)+1 = 1 - Since it's depthwise (groups=C), each output channel c depends only on input channel c. - The padded input has the value at position (1,1) and zeros elsewhere. - Kernel weight for channel c is a 3x3 matrix. - The convolution at output (0,0) is sum_{i,j} padded_input[c, i, j] * weight[c, 0, i, j]. - Only i=1, j=1 contributes because other padded values are 0. - So output[c, 0, 0] = input[c, 0, 0] * weight[c, 0, 1, 1] + bias[c]. So it IS just element-wise multiplication by the center kernel weight plus bias. And the avgpool on 1x1 is identity. So the mbconv2-5 are fully connected except for the "depthwise" which is just element-wise scale+shift. This confirms my approach. Now, for the tail kernel, I need to implement: 1. For the device-side matmul helper: ```cuda __device__ void matmul(const float* __restrict__ Wt, const float* __restrict__ b, const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ y, int M, int N) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int bsz = blockDim.x; for (int i = tid; i < M; i += bsz) { float acc = b[i]; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += Wt[j * M + i] * x[j]; } y[i] = acc; } __syncthreads(); } ``` Note: `Wt` is stored as `(N, M)` i.e., column-major or rather `W^T` in row-major format: `Wt[j * M + i]` is the element at row j, column i of W^T, which corresponds to W[i][j]. My extraction function can index them as: ```python def extract_mbconv(seq): d = {} w, b = fuse_conv_bn(seq[0], seq[1]) # seq[0] is Conv2d(in_channels, expanded_channels, 1x1, bias=False) # Weight shape: (expanded_channels, in_channels, 1, 1) # We want W^T of shape (in_channels, expanded_channels) for x * W^T + b # But since it's 1x1 conv, the weight is (out, in, 1, 1). # For matmul y = x @ W^T + b, where x is (1, in) and y is (1, out): # W^T should be (in, out). d['expand_w'] = w.squeeze().t().contiguous() # (in, out) d['expand_b'] = b # (out,) w, b = fuse_conv_bn(seq[3], seq[4]) # seq[3] is depthwise Conv2d(expanded, expanded, 3x3, groups=expanded, bias=False) # Weight shape: (expanded, 1, 3, 3) # For 1x1 input, only the center element matters: w[:, 0, 1, 1] d['dw_w'] = w[:, 0, 1, 1].contiguous() d['dw_b'] = b # SE layer 1: seq[7] is Conv2d(expanded, expanded//4, 1x1, bias=False) w = seq[7].weight.data.squeeze() # shape (expanded//4, expanded) # We want W^T of shape (expanded, expanded//4) d['se1_w'] = w.t().contiguous() d['se1_b'] = torch.zeros(seq[7].out_channels, device=seq[7].weight.device, dtype=seq[7].weight.dtype) # ReLU is seq[8] # SE layer 2: seq[9] is Conv2d(expanded//4, expanded, 1x1, bias=False) w = seq[9].weight.data.squeeze() d['se2_w'] = w.t().contiguous() d['se2_b'] = torch.zeros(seq[9].out_channels, device=seq[9].weight.device, dtype=seq[9].weight.dtype) # Output conv: seq[11] is Conv2d(expanded, out_channels, 1x1, bias=False) # BN is seq[12] w, b = fuse_conv_bn(seq[11], seq[12]) d['out_w'] = w.squeeze().t().contiguous() d['out_b'] = b return d ``` This looks correct. For the `final` conv: `ref.conv_final` is Conv2d(384, 1408, 1x1, bias=False), `ref.bn_final` is BN(1408). Fused weight `w` shape: (1408, 384, 1, 1). `w.squeeze().t()` -> (384, 1408). Correct. For the elementwise: ```cuda __device__ void elemwise_mul_add_relu(const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ b, const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ y, int M, int tid, int bsz) { for (int i = tid; i < M; i += bsz) { y[i] = fmaxf(x[i] * w[i] + b[i], 0.0f); } __syncthreads(); } ``` For sigmoid: ```cuda __device__ void matmul_sigmoid(...) { y[i] = sigmoidf(acc); } ``` For plain matmul (no activation): ```cuda __device__ void matmul(...) { y[i] = acc; } ``` And then in the kernel: ```cuda float* x = buf1; float* y = buf2; float* tmp; // avgpool into x // ... matmul_relu(se1_w, se1_b, x, y, 24, 96, tid, bsz); tmp=x; x=y; y=tmp; matmul_sigmoid(se2_w, se2_b, x, y, 96, 24, tid, bsz); tmp=x; x=y; y=tmp; matmul(out1_w, out1_b, x, y, 96, 96, tid, bsz); tmp=x; x=y; y=tmp; // ... and so on ``` This is clean. For positive numbers, sequential summation error is roughly `O(N * eps * sum)`, and for N=12544, eps=1e-7, sum=6272, error ~ 12544 * 1e-7 * 6272 ≈ 78. Wait, that seems too high. Actually, the standard error bound for sequential summation is `N * eps * S` where S is the true sum? No, the relative error bound is `N * eps`. Absolute error bound is `N * eps * S`. S=6000, N=12544, eps~1e-7, so error ~ 7.5. That's huge! But this is a worst-case bound. In practice for uniform [0,1), the partial sums grow to ~6000, and adding a ~0.5 number to ~6000 loses about log2(6000/0.5) ≈ 13.5 bits of precision, leaving about 10 bits (~3 decimal digits). bn.running_var\n gamma = bn.weight.data\n beta = bn.bias.data\n eps = bn.eps\n std = torch.sqrt(var + eps)\n w_new = w * (gamma / std).view(-1, 1, 1, 1)\n b_new = beta - mean * gamma / std\n return reasoning
The compilation passed. Now I will run the correctness check to see if this works. I also need to make sure we handle the `inplace=True` properly and that avgpool is handled correctly. One concern: the avgpool in mbconv1 happens on a per-image, per-channel basis. My current implementation does `F.adaptive_avg_pool2d(x, (1, 1))` on the output of `F.relu(self.dw1(x))`, and then the CUDA kernel reads from a flattened `(B, 96)` tensor. That matches the reference's `nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1, 1))` inside `mbconv1`. But wait - in the reference, after the avgpool in mbconv1, the subsequent layers in the mbconv use the avgpool to compute SE weights, which are then multiplied back to the feature map BEFORE the avgpool. Let's trace the reference carefully. Reference `mbconv1`: 1. expand conv (1x1) 2. bn 3. relu 4. depthwise conv (3x3) 5. bn 6. relu 7. adaptive_avg_pool2d((1,1)) ---> this pools the output of step 6 8. conv (SE reduce) 9. relu 10. conv (SE expand) 11. sigmoid 12. conv (1x1 output) 13. bn In the reference, the SE block computes from the pooled features and then the sigmoid output is broadcast-multiplied back to the feature map BEFORE the avgpool? Actually, looking at the standard MBConv block structure, the SE block should multiply back to the feature map before the final 1x1 projection. But in this specific reference implementation, the SE layers are appended sequentially in the `nn.Sequential`, which means: - Step 7: avgpool to (1,1) - Step 8: SE reduce - Step 9: relu - Step 10: SE expand - Step 11: sigmoid - Then step 12 takes the sigmoid output (which is shape (B, expanded, 1, 1)) and applies a 1x1 conv. This is not the standard MBConv block. The standard block would multiply the sigmoid output with the feature map (before the final 1x1 conv). But because these are in a Sequential, the 1x1 conv receives the sigmoid output as input, not the original feature map multiplied by the sigmoid. Actually, looking more carefully at the reference code: ```python layers.append(nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1, 1))) layers.append(nn.Conv2d(expanded_channels, expanded_channels // 4, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False)) layers.append(nn.ReLU(inplace=True)) layers.append(nn.Conv2d(expanded_channels // 4, expanded_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False)) layers.append(nn.Sigmoid()) # Output phase layers.append(nn.Conv2d(expanded_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False)) layers.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels)) ``` In the Sequential, the flow is: 1. The feature map goes through avgpool -> (B, C, 1, 1) 2. Then through SE reduce conv -> (B, C/4, 1, 1) 3. ReLU 4. SE expand conv -> (B, C, 1, 1) 5. Sigmoid 6. Output conv: takes (B, C, 1, 1) and outputs (B, out_channels, 1, 1) 7. BN This is NOT the standard SE block where we multiply the sigmoid with the original feature map. Instead, the entire block after avgpool is just a tiny MLP that reduces spatial dimensions to 1x1 and then maps channels. So the entire "tail" of mbconv is just a sequence of 1x1 convolutions on 1x1 spatial data (plus the avgpool which reduces spatial size). My optimized model: - Runs `expand1` (1x1 conv) - Runs `dw1` (3x3 depthwise conv) - Runs `F.relu` - Runs `F.adaptive_avg_pool2d` to get (B, 96, 1, 1) - Flattens to (B, 96) - Then calls the CUDA kernel which does all the remaining linear/matrix operations This matches the reference exactly because after the avgpool, everything is just 1x1 convs on 1x1 data, which is equivalent to matrix multiplication. Wait, one difference: in the reference, the `nn.Sigmoid()` outputs values that are then fed into the `nn.Conv2d`. But there's no multiplication with the original feature maps. So my approach is correct: avgpool -> then just linear layers. But I need to double check: does my `se1_w` and `se1_b` correspond to the correct weights? Let me trace: - In mbconv1: layers are [expand_conv, bn, relu, dw_conv, bn, relu, avgpool, se_reduce_conv, relu, se_expand_conv, sigmoid, output_conv, bn] - Indices: 0=expand_conv, 1=bn, 2=relu, 3=dw_conv, 4=bn, 5=relu, 6=avgpool, 7=se_reduce_conv, 8=relu, 9=se_expand_conv, 10=sigmoid, 11=output_conv, 12=bn Wait, in my `extract_mbconv`, I used: ``` w, b = fuse_conv_bn(seq[0], seq[1]) d['expand_w'] = w.squeeze().t().contiguous() ... w, b = fuse_conv_bn(seq[3], seq[4]) ... d['se1_w'] = seq[7].weight.data.squeeze().t().contiguous() d['se2_w'] = seq[9].weight.data.squeeze().t().contiguous() w, b = fuse_conv_bn(seq[11], seq[12]) ``` Yes, that looks correct. `se1_w` corresponds to `seq[7]` (SE reduce conv with kernel size 1), `se2_w` corresponds to `seq[9]` (SE expand conv), `out1_w` corresponds to `seq[11]` fused with `seq[12]`. And in my forward: - se1: matmul_relu(24, 96) - se2: matmul_sigmoid(96, 24) - out1: matmul(96, 96) This matches the dimensions in the reference: 96 -> 24 -> 96 -> 96 output. Let me run correctness check.
call → run_correctness
